import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 3D convolution with square input and square kernel.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        output_padding (int, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to 0.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 3D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 48
out_channels = 48
kernel_size = 3
depth = 64
height = 64
width = 64

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

Let me analyze the problem. I need to write a custom CUDA kernel for transposed 3D convolution (ConvTranspose3d) that is faster than the PyTorch reference. The test parameters are: - batch_size = 8 - in_channels = 48 - out_channels = 48 - kernel_size = 3 - depth = height = width = 64 - stride = 1, padding = 0, output_padding = 0, groups = 1, bias = False For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, it is actually equivalent to a regular 3D convolution (the "transpose" part doesn't change the operation when stride=1, padding=0). Wait, let me verify: For ConvTranspose3d: - output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding - For stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0: - output_size = (input_size - 1) * 1 - 0 + kernel_size + 0 = input_size - 1 + kernel_size With input_size=64, kernel_size=3, output_size = 64 - 1 + 3 = 66 So the output will be 8 x 48 x 66 x 66 x 66. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, it's mathematically equivalent to convolving the input with the weight, but the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K). In standard Conv3d, weight is (out_channels, in_channels, K, K, K). For ConvTranspose3d, the operation is like a cross-correlation with the transposed filter. Actually, when stride=1, padding=0, ConvTranspose3d is equivalent to a regular 3D convolution with appropriately flipped weights and swapping in/out channels. But the easiest approach is to implement the direct convolution computation. Let me think about how to implement this efficiently. For a direct 3D convolution (which ConvTranspose3d with stride=1 effectively is), each output element is computed as: y[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx W[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * x[b, ic, oz + kz - K + 1, oy + ky - K + 1, ox + kx - K + 1] For K=3, stride=1, padding=0, the output size is D+K-1 = 66. Wait, that's not quite right for ConvTranspose3d. Let me look up the exact formula. For ConvTranspose3d: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, (oz - kz) / stride, (oy - ky) / stride, (ox - kx) / stride] where the division is only valid when the indices are exact integers, and we only sum over valid positions. Actually, the common implementation of ConvTranspose3d internally uses the "dilated convolution" approach or computes it as a direct sum. For stride=1, padding=0: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz=0..K-1 sum_ky=0..K-1 sum_kx=0..K-1 weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, oz - kz + (K-1), oy - ky + (K-1), ox - kx + (K-1)] Wait, for ConvTranspose3d with padding=0, the relationship is: If Conv3d maps from D_out to D_in with kernel K, stride S, padding 0, then: D_in = floor((D_out - K) / S) + 1 For ConvTranspose3d, it's the inverse. To keep it simple, for stride=1: D_out = D_in + K - 1 And the operation is: for each output position (oz, oy, ox): output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = 0 for each kernel position (kz, ky, kx): iz = oz - (K - 1 - kz) // or some variant if iz is in range: output += weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * x[b, ic, iz, iy, ix] Let me be more careful. For standard Conv3d: y[b, oc, i, j, k] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx W[oc, ic, kz, ky, kx] * x[b, ic, i*S + kz - P, j*S + ky - P, k*S + kx - P] For ConvTranspose3d, we want the adjoint operation. With stride=1: x[b, ic, i, j, k] = sum_oc sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx W[oc, ic, kz, ky, kx] * y[b, oc, i - kz + P, j - ky + P, k - kx + P] So: y[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] contribution from x[b, ic, iz, iy, ix] at position (kz, ky, kx): x[b, ic, iz, iy, ix] * W[oc, ic, kz, ky, kx] where iz = oz - kz + P, iy = oy - ky + P, ix = ox - kx + P So oz = iz + kz - P, and P=0 here. Hmm wait, ConvTranspose3d in PyTorch uses weight shape (in_channels, out_channels // groups, K, K, K). For groups=1, it's (in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K). Let me think about this differently. If standard conv maps input of size N to output of size M, then transpose conv maps output size M back to input size N. For standard conv with stride=1, kernel=K, padding=0: M = N - K + 1 For transpose conv: N = M - K + 1, so M = N + K - 1. And: input is size M, output is size N. For transpose conv, if we think of it as the gradient of standard conv: Let standard conv be Y = X * W (with appropriate padding). Then transpose conv computes the "adjoint": given dY, compute dX = dY * W^T. In the forward direction of transpose conv, we have: output = conv_transpose3d(input, weight) where input is what would be the "output gradient" in backprop, and weight is the same. For stride=1, padding=0, kernel=K: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx W[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, oz + (K-1-kz), oy + (K-1-ky), ox + (K-1-kx)] where the input indices need to be valid. Wait, no that's if it were flipped. Let me double check the PyTorch source or use an example. Actually, let me just verify with PyTorch: For a simple 1D case with kernel=[w0, w1] and input=[x0, x1]: ConvTranspose1d with padding=0, stride=1 would give: output[0] = x0 * w0 output[1] = x0 * w1 + x1 * w0 output[2] = x1 * w1 So output[i+j] += input[i] * weight[j]. For 3D, output[oz, oy, ox] += input[iz, iy, ix] * weight[kz, ky, kx] where oz = iz + kz, oy = iy + ky, ox = ix + kx. And weight here is weight[oc, ic, kz, ky, kx]... wait no, for ConvTranspose3d, weight is (in_channels, out_channels, *kernel_size). For a dot product view: weight[oc, ic] for standard conv, weight[ic, oc] for transpose conv. Wait, let me check PyTorch. In nn.ConvTranspose3d, weight has shape (in_channels, out_channels // groups, *kernel_size). And the operation is: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, iz, iy, ix] where oz = iz + (K-1-kz), oy = iy + (K-1-ky), ox = ix + (K-1-kx)... wait. Actually from the 1D example: If weight = [w0, w1] and input = [x0, x1], output = [x0*w0, x0*w1 + x1*w0, x1*w1]. So output_k = sum_i input_i * weight_{k-i} where we pad with zeros outside [0, len(weight)-1]. This is cross-correlation, not convolution. So: output_k = sum_j input_{k-j} * weight_j, where weight_j = 0 for j < 0 or j >= K. For ConvTranspose, with stride=1, padding=0: output_{i+j} += input_i * weight_j. The weight[ic, oc, j] in PyTorch maps to this. So: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] += sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, oz-kz, oy-ky, ox-kx] where the input coordinates must be valid (0 <= oz-kz < D_in, etc.). Wait, but in PyTorch ConvTranspose3d, they also do dilation of the input. For stride > 1, they insert zeros between input elements. For stride=1: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, (oz-kz)/S, (oy-ky)/S, (ox-kx)/S] when the divisions are exact integers. For S=1: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, oz-kz, oy-ky, ox-kx] where oz-kz, oy-ky, ox-kx must be in [0, D_in-1], and weight[...] is the transposed conv weight. But this formula doesn't account for padding. With padding P, the effective formula for the input to standard conv would pack more, so for transpose conv with padding P: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, (oz-kz+P)/S, ...] where valid. For S=1, P=0: output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox] = sum_ic sum_kz sum_ky sum_kx weight[ic, oc, kz, ky, kx] * input[b, ic, oz-kz, oy-ky, ox-kx] valid when 0 <= oz-kz < D_in, 0 <= oy-ky < H_in, 0 <= ox-kx < W_in. Given D_in=64, K=3, output size = 64+3-1 = 66. For oz=0: kz must be 0, and input index is 0. For oz=1: kz can be 0 (input=1) or 1 (input=0). For oz=2: kz can be 0 (input=2), 1 (input=1), 2 (input=0). So the sum ranges over kz from max(0, oz - 63) to min(2, oz). Actually, kz: 0 <= oz - kz < 64 => oz - 64 < kz <= oz => max(0, oz - 63) <= kz <= min(2, oz). Let me verify: for oz=65, kz: max(0, 65-63)=2 to min(2, 65)=2, so only kz=2, input=63. Correct. Now, how to implement this efficiently on GPU? The dimensions are: - Batch: 8 - In channels: 48 - Out channels: 48 - Kernel: 3x3x3 - Input spatial: 64x64x64 - Output spatial: 66x66x66 Total output elements: 8 * 48 * 66^3 = 8 * 48 * 287496 = 110,398,464. Each output element requires summing over: - in_channels * K^3 = 48 * 27 = 1,296 multiplications. So total FLOPs = 110,398,464 * 1,296 ≈ 143 GFLOPs. For FP32 on H100, peak is 67 TFLOPS. But we're memory bound since each output requires reading 48 * 27 = 1,296 floats ≈ 5.2 KB of weight and some input, but weights are reused across spatial positions. Actually, if we tile properly, weights can be kept in shared memory/registers, and we can achieve good compute utilization. But the problem is relatively small. Let's think about the memory footprint: - Input: 8 * 48 * 64^3 = 12,582,912 floats ≈ 48.4 MB - Weight: 48 * 48 * 27 = 62,208 floats ≈ 240 KB - Output: 8 * 48 * 66^3 = 13,167,744 floats ≈ 50.2 MB So the total memory movement is about 48.4 + 50.2 + 0.24 ≈ 98.8 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, this would take about 29 microseconds if perfectly memory-bound. But convolutions are compute-bound or have good caching. Actually, we need to think about this differently. PyTorch uses cuDNN which is highly optimized. To beat it, we need to find an approach that works well for these specific parameters. For small kernels and small channel counts, a direct convolution with explicit unrolling might work well. Key insight: The kernel size is only 3x3x3, which is very small. For each output position, we only need to access a 3x3x3 neighborhood of input corresponding to each input channel. Actually, for a single output channel and single batch element, each output position (oz, oy, ox) is computed from a 3x3x3 region of each input channel. Given the small K=3, we can compute multiple output channels per thread block to reuse the input data. Let me think about tiling strategies: **Approach 1: Tile over output spatial and channels** - Each thread block handles a tile of output spatial positions (e.g., 8x8x8) and some output channels. - We load a tile of input that covers the required footprint. - For K=3, an 8x8x8 output tile requires a 10x10x10 input tile (considering the overlap). - Shared memory needed for input: 10^3 * tile_in_channels * 4 bytes. - If we process 8 output channels per block and 8 input channels per iteration: - Input tile in SM: 10*10*10*8*4 = 32,000 bytes - Weight in SM or registers: 8*8*27*4 = 6,912 bytes - Total: manageable. But wait, for 48 in_channels and 48 out_channels, we can process all of them in groups. **Approach 2: Use gemm-based (im2col) approach** - Unfold the input into columns: for each spatial position, extract the 3x3x3 neighborhood. - This creates a matrix of size (64*64*64) x (48*27). - Multiply by weight matrix of size (48*27) x 48. - Result is (64*64*64) x 48, which is reshaped to output. - But this is exactly what PyTorch likely does internally via cuDNN. To beat cuDNN, we should exploit specific sizes and avoid overheads. For K=3, we might implement a direct convolution that's faster due to avoiding im2col overhead. **Approach 3: FFT-based** - For K=3 and output size 66, FFT of size ~66-128 is not worth it due to overhead. Let me try a direct convolution kernel. Thread organization: - Let's say 256 threads per block. - We can divide the 8x8x4 spatial tile into 256 threads: e.g., threadIdx.x ∈ [0,7], threadIdx.y ∈ [0,7], threadIdx.z ∈ [0,3], but CUDA blocks are 1D typically. So weight[in_ch, :, kz, ky, kx] is a contiguous vector of 8 floats if we store weight as (in_ch, oc, kz, ky, kx) and oc is contiguous (oc varies fastest). Actually, PyTorch stores ConvTranspose3d weight as (in_channels, out_channels, kD, kH, kW). So oc is the second dimension. If we access weight[in_ch, :, kz, ky, kx], that's strided by kD*kH*kW = 27 elements. Not contiguous in PyTorch's default layout. But in the CUDA kernel, we can read it and store it in shared memory in a favorable layout. We can transpose to have (in_ch, kz, ky, kx, oc) with oc contiguous. So in shared memory for weights: - Layout: w_smem[in_ch][kz][ky][kx][oc] where oc ∈ [0,7]. - Each thread can load a few elements. Let me think about bank conflicts. H100 has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. A warp is 32 threads. If 32 threads read adjacent floats, that's broadcast to different banks, no conflict. If we store input as [in_ch][z][y][x], and adjacent threads want x+1, then: - Thread 0 wants [ic][z][y][0] - Thread 1 wants [ic][z][y][1] - These are adjacent in memory, so they map to adjacent banks: no conflict (each float is 4 bytes, each bank is 4 bytes, so bank(i) = (addr / 4) % 32). - Wait: bank = (byte_address / 4) % 32. So 32 consecutive floats map to 32 consecutive banks. Perfect! For weights: we want [in_ch][kz][ky][kx][oc] with oc contiguous. Let me reconsider the thread mapping. Another option: - 256 threads per block. - Block handles 8 spatial positions × 32 oc? No, too many ocs. - How about: warp handles 1 oc × 32 spatial positions? So: - 8 warps × 32 threads each = 256 threads. - Warp w handles oc = w (relative to block's oc_base). - Within warp, thread t handles spatial position t. - Total spatial positions: 32 per warp, 8 warps → 256 spatial positions. - Spatial tile: 8×8×4 = 256 positions. Perfect. And they access different spatial positions, so input accesses are to adjacent memory locations if spatial positions are adjacent. But 32 spatial positions... if our tile is 8x8x4 = 256, we can divide into 8 warps of 32 threads. How to assign 32 positions per warp? - Positions are arranged as linear index from 0 to 255. Warp w gets positions w*32 + lane. - Adjacent lanes get adjacent linear indices. If we store input with x varying fastest, and the 32 positions span a small region, there'll be good spatial locality. Wait, is ConvTranspose3d with stride=1 equivalent to Conv3d with appropriate padding and flipped channels/weights? For standard Conv3d with stride=1, kernel=3, padding=1: output size = input size. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, kernel=3, padding=0: output size = input size + 2. So it's like convolving a zero-padded input. But the weight orientation matters. In standard conv, weight is (out, in, K, K, K). In transposed conv, weight is (in, out, K, K, K). The transposed conv operation for stride=1 is essentially a cross-correlation (like conv) with weight shape swapped, but without flipping the kernel. Actually, if we define W_conv[oc, ic] = W_tconv[ic, oc], then conv_transpose3d(x, W_tconv) for stride=1 is equivalent to padding x with K-1 zeros and then doing conv3d(x_padded, W_conv). Let me verify this with a simple 1D example: - Conv1d with kernel=[w0, w1], stride=1: y[i] = w0*x[i] + w1*x[i+1] (with proper boundaries). Wait, actually conv1d (cross-correlation) is y[i] = sum_k w[k] * x[i+k]. With kernel [w0,w1]: y[0] = w0*x[0] + w1*x[1], y[1] = w0*x[1] + w1*x[2]. Output size = N-1. - ConvTranspose1d is the adjoint: given dY of size N-1, compute dX of size N. dX[i] = w0*dY[i-1] + w1*dY[i] (with boundaries, dY[-1]=0). dX[0] = w1*dY[0] dX[1] = w0*dY[0] + w1*dY[1] dX[2] = w0*dY[1] So conv_transpose1d with kernel [w0,w1] and input [y0,y1] gives output [w1*y0, w0*y0+w1*y1, w0*y1]. Now, conv1d with kernel [w1,w0] (flipped) and zero-padded input [0, y0, y1, 0]: y_padded = [0, y0, y1, 0] Using conv (cross-correlation): z[i] = w1*y_padded[i] + w0*y_padded[i+1]. z[0] = w1*0 + w0*y0 = w0*y0. Hmm, not matching. If we use kernel [w0, w1] without flip: z[0] = w0*0 + w1*y0 = w1*y0. Matches dX[0]. z[1] = w0*y0 + w1*y1. Matches dX[1]. z[2] = w0*y1 + w1*0 = w0*y1. Matches dX[2]. So conv_transpose1d with weight [w0,w1] is equivalent to conv1d with the SAME weight [w0,w1] on zero-padded input! For 3D: ConvTranspose3d with weight (in, out, K,K,K) and stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to Conv3d with weight (out, in, K,K,K) = transpose(weight_tconv, 0, 1) applied to zero-padded input. So ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0 is mathematically equivalent to: 1. Pad input with K-1 zeros on all spatial sides. 2. Apply a standard Conv3d with weight shape (out_channels, in_channels, K, K, K) that is the transpose of the original weight: W_conv[oc, ic] = W_tconv[ic, oc]. 3. Another idea: for K=3, I can unroll the K loops fully (27 iterations) and compute each output element with 48*27=1296 ops. A thread can compute multiple output elements. Given that K=3 is small, a good approach is: - Each thread computes one output pixel for multiple output channels. - A warp computes a tile of output spatial positions for multiple output channels. - We use warp-level accumulation and load weights into shared memory. Let me verify this with PyTorch. In 1D: conv_transpose1d with kernel [w0,w1] and input [x0,x1] gives: z[0] = w0 * 0 + w1 * x0 = w1*x0 z[1] = w0 * x0 + w1 * x1 z[2] = w0 * x1 + w1 * 0 = w0*x1 conv1d with kernel [a,b] on padded input [0,x0,x1,0]: z[0] = a*0 + b*x0 = b*x0 z[1] = a*x0 + b*x1 z[2] = a*x1 + b*0 = a*x1 So a = w0, b = w1. In 3D: for conv3d, out[0,0,0] = w[2,2,2] * x_padded[2,2,2] if we use the kernel as-is. But conv_transpose wants w[0,0,0] * x[0,0,0]. But note that PyTorch's conv3d performs cross-correlation, not convolution. In cross-correlation, the kernel is NOT flipped. For standard conv layers, what PyTorch calls "conv" is actually cross-correlation. Let me verify: PyTorch conv3d: out[n,i,j,k] = sum_w * weight[:,i,w1,w2,w3] * input[:,j,j+w1,k+w2,l+w3]. No flipping. Let me write a straightforward cuda kernel that computes the output using the direct formula: ``` for b, oc, oz, oy, ox: sum = 0 for ic in range(IC): for kz in range(KD): iz = oz - kz if iz < 0 or iz >= ID: continue for ky in range(KH): iy = oy - ky if iy < 0 or iy >= IH: continue for kx in range(KW): ix = ox - kx if ix < 0 or ix >= IW: continue sum += weight[ic][oc][kz][ky][kx] * input[b][ic][iz][iy][ix] output[b][oc][oz][oy][ox] = sum ``` But this is very branchy. For boundary cases, some threads will take fewer iterations. But for interior threads where oz∈[2,65], etc., all k values are valid. Let me compute the output size: 66. For K=3, the boundary where not all 3 k values are valid: - oz=0: only kz=0 - oz=1: kz=0,1 - oz=64: kz=0,1,2 but iz=64,63,62. kz=2 gives iz=62 valid. All valid. Just 1. - oz=1: 0 to 1. 2 values. - oz>=2 and oz<=63: 0 to 2. 3 values. - oz=64: max(0,1)=1 to min(2,64)=2. 2 values. - oz=65: max(0,2)=2 to min(2,65)=2. 1 value. So boundary effects only happen on 4 out of 66 positions. Most positions (62/66 = 94%) have all 27 iterations valid. We can handle boundaries with conditionals; the divergence is minimal. Another formula that avoids boundaries is the padded-input conv3d: Pad input with 2 zeros, then do a full 3D conv where all k values are always valid for relevant output positions. But then we'd need a 68×68×68 input, which is larger but simplifies boundary handling. Actually, 68*68*68 = 314,432 vs 64*64*64 = 262,144. Not much larger. Let me write the kernel using the padded input approach for simplicity inside the main loop: - Before the main compute, allocate a zero-padded input of size (B, IC, D+2, H+2, W+2). - Copy input to the center. - Then the kernel can access without bounds checks. I really want to try a kernel that stores weights in shared memory and processes tiles. Here's a concrete kernel design I'm going to try: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int OC, int D, int H, int W, int KD, int KH, int KW, int OD, int OH, int OW ) { // Block organization // blockIdx.x: spatial block index (linearized over oz, oy, ox tiles) // blockIdx.y: batch index // blockIdx.z: output channel group (0..OC/8-1) // Tile dimensions: 4x4x4 spatial, 8 output channels // 256 threads: each thread handles 1 output spatial position and 1 output channel. // Wait, 4*4*4=64 spatial positions * 8 oc = 512 work items. With 256 threads, each thread handles 2 output channels for 1 spatial position. // Let me change: 8 output channels, 32 spatial positions per block. // blockDim.x = 256. // 256 threads = 8 warps * 32. // Warp i handles oc = oc_start + i, and 32 spatial positions. // Total spatial per block = 8 warps * 32 = 256 positions = 8*8*4. // Actually, let's use 8x8x4 tile. int oc_start = blockIdx.z * 8; int b = blockIdx.y; int spatial_tiles_x = (OW + 3) / 4; int spatial_tiles_y = (OH + 7) / 8; int spatial_tiles_z = (OD + 7) / 8; int total_spatial_tiles = spatial_tiles_x * spatial_tiles_y * spatial_tiles_z; // But blockIdx.x ranges over spatial tiles. int tile_id = blockIdx.x; // Wait, I said blockIdx.x is spatial. // Actually: launch with grid(1,1,1) and compute inside? No. // Grid should be (spatial_tiles, B, OC/8). int tile_x = tile_id % spatial_tiles_x; int tile_y = (tile_id / spatial_tiles_x) % spatial_tiles_y; int tile_z = tile_id / (spatial_tiles_x * spatial_tiles_y); int oz_base = tile_z * 8; int oy_base = tile_y * 8; int ox_base = tile_x * 4; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int oc = oc_start + warp_id; // but only if oc < OC if (oc >= OC) return; // boundary int spatial_idx = lane + warp_id * 32; // 0..255 // Wait, if warp_id handles oc, then lane should handle spatial_idx = (warp_id * 32 + lane)? // But warp_id determines oc. So warp w handles oc=oc_start+w and spatial positions w*32 to w*32+31. // For 8x8x4=256 tile, spatial_idx = warp_id * 32 + lane gives 0..255. int os = spatial_idx; int dz = os / 64; int dy = (os / 8) % 8; int dx = os % 8; int oz = oz_base + dz; int oy = oy_base + dy; int ox = ox_base + dx; // Now, compute output[b,oc,oz,oy,ox] // Iterate over ic and kz,ky,kx. float sum = 0.0f; // Process input channel groups for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += 8) { // Shared memory for input and weights // Let's load input for this tile and ic group to shared memory. // Input footprint: oz in [oz_base, oz_base+7], oy in [oy_base, oy_base+7], ox in [ox_base, ox_base+7] // Plus K-1 = 2 on each side. // But we need input at [ic, oz-kz, oy-ky, ox-kx]. // Since oz-kz ranges from oz_base-2 to oz_base+7, etc. // Dynamic smem extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* in_smem = smem; // [8][10][10][10] float* w_smem = smem + 8*10*10*10; // [8][8][3][3][3] // Load input to shared memory // Each thread loads some elements int in_total = 8 * 10 * 10 * 10; // ic_group * footprint for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < in_total; i += blockDim.x) { int ic_offset = i / 1000; int rem = i % 1000; int iz = rem / 100; int rem2 = rem % 100; int iy = rem2 / 10; int ix = rem2 % 10; int global_iz = oz_base + iz - 2; // because footprint starts at oz_base-2 int global_iy = oy_base + iy - 2; int global_ix = ox_base + ix - 2; float val = 0.0f; if (global_iz >= 0 && global_iz < D && global_iy >= 0 && global_iy < H && global_ix >= 0 && global_ix < W) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_offset) * D + global_iz) * H * W + global_iy * W + global_ix]; } in_smem[i] = val; } // Load weights to shared memory // w_smem[ic][oc_local][kz][ky][kx] int w_total = 8 * 8 * 27; // ic_group * oc_group * K^3 for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < w_total; i += blockDim.x) { int ic_offset = i / (8*27); int rem = i % (8*27); int oc_local = rem / 27; int k = rem % 27; int kz = k / 9; int rem2 = k % 9; int ky = rem2 / 3; int kx = rem2 % 3; if (oc_start + oc_local < OC && ic_base + ic_offset < IC) { w_smem[i] = weight[((ic_base + ic_offset) * OC + oc_start + oc_local) * 27 + k]; } else { w_smem[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Now compute for this ic_group // Each thread (oc=oc_start+warp_id) computes its spatial position if (oz < OD && oy < OH && ox < OW) { for (int ic_rel = 0; ic_rel < 8 && ic_base + ic_rel < IC; ++ic_rel) { for (int kz = 0; kz < 3; ++kz) { int iz_smem = dz + 2 - kz; // because smem is centered... wait. // smem layout: [ic_rel][iz][iy][ix] where iz in [0,9], etc. // global_iz = oz_base + iz - 2. // We want iz such that global_iz = oz - kz = oz_base + dz - kz. // So iz = dz - kz + 2. int iz_s = dz - kz + 2; for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ++ky) { int iy_s = dy - ky + 2; for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; ++kx) { int ix_s = dx - kx + 2; float in_val = in_smem[((ic_rel * 10 + iz_s) * 10 + iy_s) * 10 + ix_s]; float w_val = w_smem[((ic_rel * 8 + warp_id) * 9 + kz * 3 + ky) * 3 + kx]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (oz < OD && oy < OH && ox < OW && oc < OC) { output[((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH * OW + oy * OW + ox] = sum; } } ``` There are several issues with this: 1. `blockIdx.x` should map properly. But gridDim.x might not be `total_spatial_tiles` unless I set it that way. 2. `spatial_idx = warp_id * 32 + lane` ensures each warp handles 32 consecutive spatial positions. But this requires `warp_id` to be related to the oc within the block. 3. The thread mapping assumes `warp_id` goes from 0 to 7 for the 8 output channels. But `spatial_idx = warp_id * 32 + lane` means warp 0 handles spatial positions 0..31, warp 1 handles 32..63, etc. This means different warps handle different spatial positions, not the same spatial positions for different ocs. That's actually a problem! I wanted each warp to handle a fixed oc but a subset of spatial positions. But the way I wrote the addressing, `oc = oc_start + warp_id`, so warp 0 computes oc=oc_start for positions 0..31, warp 1 computes oc=oc_start+1 for positions 32..63, etc. This means each warp computes a different oc for a DIFFERENT spatial patch. Here's the kernel I'm going to write: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int OC, int ID, int IH, int IW, int KD, int KH, int KW, int OD, int OH, int OW ) { const int TX = 8; const int TY = 8; const int TZ = 4; const int OC_TILE = 8; const int IC_TILE = 8; const int K = 3; int oc_start = blockIdx.z * OC_TILE; int b = blockIdx.y; int tiles_x = (OW + TX - 1) / TX; int tiles_y = (OH + TY - 1) / TY; int tiles_z = (OD + TZ - 1) / TZ; int tile_id = blockIdx.x; int tile_x = tile_id % tiles_x; int tile_y = (tile_id / tiles_x) % tiles_y; int tile_z = tile_id / (tiles_x * tiles_y); int ox_base = tile_x * TX; int oy_base = tile_y * TY; int oz_base = tile_z * TZ; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int oc = oc_start + warp_id; int dz = warp_id / 2; int dy = (warp_id % 2) * 4 + (lane / 8); int dx = lane % 8; int ox = ox_base + dx; int oy = oy_base + dy; int oz = oz_base + dz; // Shared memory layout // Input footprint: TZ+2 in z, TY+2 in y, TX+2 in x const int FZ = TZ + 2; // 6 const int FY = TY + 2; // 10 const int FX = TX + 2; // 10 extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* in_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX; float accum = 0.0f; // Iterate over input channels in groups for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input footprint int in_total = IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < in_total; i += blockDim.x) { int ic_off = i / (FZ * FY * FX); int rem = i % (FZ * FY * FX); int iz = rem / (FY * FX); int rem2 = rem % (FY * FX); int iy = rem2 / FX; int ix = rem2 % FX; int g_iz = oz_base + iz - (KD - 1); int g_iy = oy_base + iy - (KH - 1); int g_ix = ox_base + ix - (KW - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (g_iz >= 0 && g_iz < ID && g_iy >= 0 && g_iy < IH && g_ix >= 0 && g_ix < IW) { val = input[(((b * IC + ic_base + ic_off) * ID + g_iz) * IH + g_iy) * IW + g_ix]; } in_smem[i] = val; } // Load weights int w_total = IC_TILE * OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < w_total; i += blockDim.x) { int ic_off = i / (OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW); int rem = i % (OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW); int oc_off = rem / (KD * KH * KW); int k = rem % (KD * KH * KW); int global_oc = oc_start + oc_off; int global_ic = ic_base + ic_off; float val = 0.0f; if (global_oc < OC && global_ic < IC) { val = weight[((global_ic * OC + global_oc) * KD + k / (KH * KW)) * KH * KW + k % (KH * KW)]; // Actually need to decompose k into kz, ky, kx } w_smem[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute if valid output position if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD && oc < OC) { for (int ic_off = 0; ic_off < IC_TILE && ic_base + ic_off < IC; ++ic_off) { for (int kz = 0; kz < KD; ++kz) { int iz_s = dz + (KD - 1) - kz; for (int ky = 0; ky < KH; ++ky) { int iy_s = dy + (KH - 1) - ky; for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix_s = dx + (KW - 1) - kx; float in_val = in_smem[((ic_off * FZ + iz_s) * FY + iy_s) * FX + ix_s]; // Weight layout in smem: [ic_off][oc_off][kz][ky][kx] float w_val = w_smem[(((ic_off * OC_TILE + warp_id) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx]; accum += in_val * w_val; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD && oc < OC) { output[(((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH + oy) * OW + ox] = accum; } } ``` Wait, I need to fix the weight indexing. The original weight layout is (IC, OC, KD, KH, KW). weight[global_ic, global_oc, kz, ky, kx] in C-contiguous indexing: idx = ((global_ic * OC + global_oc) * KD + kz) * KH * KW + ky * KW + kx. For w_smem, the k indexing in the load loop: k ranges 0..KD*KH*KW-1. kz = k / (KH * KW) rem = k % (KH * KW) ky = rem / KW kx = rem % KW Let me fix the load loop: ```cuda int k = rem % (KD * KH * KW); int kz = k / (KH * KW); int krem = k % (KH * KW); int ky = krem / KW; int kx = krem % KW; int weight_idx = ((global_ic * OC + global_oc) * KD + kz) * KH * KW + ky * KW + kx; ``` And in the compute loop, w_smem indexing: w_smem[ic_off][warp_id][kz][ky][kx] should correspond to the linear index: idx = (((ic_off * OC_TILE + warp_id) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx. This matches the loading loop since we computed `rem` as `oc_off * KD*KH*KW + k`. So the compute loop's weight access is correct. Now for the grid launch: ```python tiles_x = (OW + TX - 1) // TX tiles_y = (OH + TY - 1) // TY tiles_z = (OD + TZ - 1) // TZ spatial_tiles = tiles_x * tiles_y * tiles_z grid = (spatial_tiles, batch_size, (out_channels + OC_TILE - 1) // OC_TILE) block = 256 smem_size = (IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX + IC_TILE * OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW) * 4 ``` For the specific test case: - OW=66, OH=66, OD=66, TX=8, TY=8, TZ=4 - tiles_x=9, tiles_y=9, tiles_z=17 - spatial_tiles=1377 - batch=8 - oc_groups=6 - Total blocks=1377*8*6=66,096 - smem_size = (8*6*10*10 + 8*8*27) * 4 = (4800 + 1728) * 4 = 26,112 bytes. int g_iz = oz_base + iz - (KD - 1); int g_iy = oy_base + iy - (KH - 1); int g_ix = ox_base + ix - (KW - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (g_iz >= 0 && g_iz < ID && g_iy >= 0 && g_iy < IH && g_ix >= 0 && g_ix < IW) { val = input[(((b * IC + ic_base + ic_off) * ID + g_iz) * IH + g_iy) * IW + g_ix]; int oc_off = rem / K_size; int k = rem % K_size; int global_oc = oc_start + oc_off; int global_ic = ic_base + ic_off; int kz = k / (KH * KW); int krem = k % (KH * KW); int ky = krem / KW; int kx = krem % KW; float val = 0.0f; if (global_oc < OC && global_ic < IC) { val = weight[(((global_ic * OC + global_oc) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx]; } w_smem[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD && oc < OC) { #pragma unroll for (int ic_off = 0; #pragma unroll for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix_s = dx + (KW - 1) - kx; float in_val = in_smem[(((ic_off * FZ + iz_s) * FY + iy_s) * FX + ix_s)]; float w_val = w_smem[((((ic_off * OC_TILE + warp_id) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx)]; accum += in_val * w_val; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD && oc < OC) { output[(((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH + oy) * OW + ox] = accum; } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int output_padding ) { // Since this is exact ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0 int B = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int ID = input.size(2); int IH = input.size(3); int IW = input.size(4); int OC = weight.size(1); int KD = weight.size(2); int KH = weight.size(3); int KW = weight.size(4); int OD = (ID - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + KD + output_padding; const int TY = 8; const int TZ = 4; const int OC_TILE = 8; const int IC_TILE = 8; int tiles_x = (OW + TX - 1) / TX; int tiles_y = (OH + TY - 1) / TY; int tiles_z = (OD + TZ - 1) / TZ; int spatial_tiles = tiles_x * tiles_y * tiles_z; dim3 grid(spatial_tiles, B, (OC + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE); dim3 block(256); const int FZ = TZ + KD - 1; const int FY = TY + KH - 1; const int FX = TX + KW - 1; size_t smem_size = (IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX + IC_TILE * OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW) * sizeof(float); Another optimization: instead of having all threads load input and weights in the same loops, maybe I can have threads in a warp cooperate to load contiguous memory. But with the current loop structure, `for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < total; i += blockDim.x)`, threadIdx.x increments by 256, so threads access memory at strides of 256 floats = 1KB. These accesses are not contiguous; they're scattered. This might hurt global memory coalescing. For input loading: `in_total = IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX` = 8 * 6 * 10 * 10 = 4,800. 4,800 / 256 = 18.75 rounds. Each thread loads 18 or 19 elements. Elements i and i+256 are 256*4 = 1,024 bytes apart. These map to different cache lines, so each thread generates many cache line requests. This is very poor coalescing! We should restructure the loading so that adjacent threads load adjacent memory. One way is to have threads load in a strided pattern where the inner dimension is contiguous. We can transpose the loading: for the input, the global memory is stored as [ic][z][y][x]. We want threads to load contiguous x values together. Given 256 threads and a footprint of 8*6*10*10 = 4,800 floats: - We can have threads load in groups of 32 along the last dimension. - Or we can have each warp load one z-slice or y-row. Actually, the simplest fix is to have thread t load element t, then t+256, etc. The issue is that t and t+1 load adjacent elements (good within a warp), but t and t+256 are far apart. However, consecutive warps will load consecutive chunks. Wait: for i = threadIdx.x + k*256: - thread 0 loads: i=0, 256, 512, ... - thread 1 loads: i=1, 257, 513, ... - Within a warp (threads 0..31), they load i=0..31 simultaneously, which are 128 contiguous bytes. That's a single memory transaction! - Then threads 0..31 load i=256..287, another contiguous segment. Another memory transaction! So the loading IS coalesced! Because threads with consecutive threadIdx.x access consecutive memory addresses simultaneously. Even though each thread later accesses i+256, that access is done after the first 32 threads have completed, and it corresponds to a different contiguous segment. Wait, no. A warp executes in lockstep. The instruction `for (i=threadIdx.x; i<total; i+=256)` means all 32 threads in a warp evaluate the condition and execute the load for i = lane + k*256. For k=0, lanes 0..31 access 0..31 (coalesced). For k=1, lanes 0..31 access 256..287 (coalesced). So yes, each iteration of the inner loop is coalesced. Perfect! So the loading is coalesced. Good. Similarly for weights: `IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_size` = 8*8*27 = 1,728. `thread 0: 0, 256, 512, 768, 1024, 1280`. These are also coalesced within each k iteration. Great, the memory loading is fine. The `accum += in_val * w_val` pattern should map to FFMA on H100. Also, with 48 input channels and unrolled k loops, each thread does 48 * 27 = 1,296 FFMAs. Let me write this baseline kernel first. ```cpp __global__ void conv_transpose3d_baseline( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int OC, int ID, int IH, int IW, int KD, int KH, int KW, int OD, int OH, int OW, int padding ) { int total_output = B * OC * OD * OH * OW; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_output) return; // Decode index int rem = idx; int b = rem / (OC * OD * OH * OW); rem %= OC * OD * OH * OW; int oc = rem / (OD * OH * OW); rem %= OD * OH * OW; int oz = rem / (OH * OW); rem %= OH * OW; int oy = rem / OW; int ox = rem % OW; float sum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kz = 0; kz < KD; ++kz) { int iz = oz - kz + (KD - 1) - padding; // Wait, need to verify // Actually for transposed conv with stride=1: // output[oz] = sum_kz input[oz - kz] * weight[kz] // where valid when 0 <= oz - kz < ID, i.e., kz <= oz < ID + kz // For oz from 0 to OD-1 = ID+KD-2: // iz = oz - kz, needs 0 <= iz < ID int iz2 = oz - kz; if (iz2 < 0 || iz2 >= ID) continue; for (int ky = 0; ky < KH; ++ky) { int iy2 = oy - ky; if (iy2 < 0 || iy2 >= IH) continue; for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix2 = ox - kx; if (ix2 < 0 || ix2 >= IW) continue; float in_val = input[(((b * IC + ic) * ID + iz2) * IH + iy2) * IW + ix2]; float w_val = weight[(((ic * OC + oc) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[(((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH + oy) * OW + ox] = sum; } ``` Wait, I need to double check the indexing for transposed conv. Actually, for transposed conv with all-ones weight and all-ones input: Each output position gets a contribution from each input position that can reach it via the kernel. With all-ones: output[d,h,w] = number of valid (kd, kh, kw) such that (d-kd, h-kh, w-kw) is in input range. For a 2×2×2 input and 3×3×3 kernel: output[0,0,0]: only (0,0,0) can reach it with (0,0,0) offset. Value = 1. output[1,1,1]: (0,0,0) with offset (1,1,1), (1,0,0) with (0,1,1), (0,1,0) with (1,0,1), etc. All 8 input positions can reach (1,1,1) via some offset. Value = 8. Actually wait, for a 2×2×2 input and 3×3×3 kernel, output is 4×4×4. Each output position gets contributions from input positions within a 3×3×3 neighborhood behind it. For position (1,1,1): - input (0,0,0) via kernel (1,1,1) - input (0,0,1) via kernel (1,1,0) - input (0,1,0) via kernel (1,0,1) - input (0,1,1) via kernel (1,0,0) - input (1,0,0) via kernel (0,1,1) - input (1,0,1) via kernel (0,1,0) - input (1,1,0) via kernel (0,0,1) - input (1,1,1) via kernel (0,0,0) So 8 contributions. Value = 8. For position (0,0,0): only input (0,0,0) via kernel (0,0,0). Value = 1. For position (3,3,3): only input (1,1,1) via kernel (2,2,2) (but wait, kernel indices are 0,1,2). Value = 1. This matches a full convolution with all-ones. So the formula output[d,h,w] = sum input[d-kd, h-kh, w-kw] * w[kd, kh, kw] IS correct for padding=0. Now what about the weight indexing? In PyTorch ConvTranspose3d, weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, KD, KH, KW). So weight[ic, oc, kd, kh, kw] is correct. My kernel had this formula in the shared memory version. But PyTorch's Conv3d uses cross-correlation (not convolution) with convention: output[d] = sum_k input[d + k - padding] * weight[k] Wait, PyTorch's Conv3d is cross-correlation: output[d, h, w] = sum_c sum_{kd,kh,kw} input[c, d + kd - p, h + kh - p, w + kw - p] * weight[c, o, kd, kh, kw] For p=0: output[d] = sum_k input[d + k] * w[k] Yes, PyTorch Conv3d uses CROSS-CORRELATION (not convolution in the signal processing sense). For ConvTranspose3d, the formula from PyTorch docs: output[d, h, w] = sum_c sum_{kd,kh,kw} input[c, d + kd - p, h + kh - p, w + kw - p] * weight[c, o, kd, kh, kw] Wait, I found conflicting info in my earlier analysis. So the formula is: output = bias + sum_k valid_3d_cross_correlation(input[:, k], weight[k, :]) For 3D cross-correlation with padding (in the transposed conv): output[d] += input[d + kd - p] * weight[kd] So the actual computation is: output[d] = sum_k input[d + k - p] * weight[k] For p=0: output[d] = sum_k input[d + k] * weight[k] Let me verify with my earlier test: For input size 2, kernel 3, all ones: output[0] = input[0]*w[0] + input[1]*w[1] + input[2]*w[2] = 1 + 1 + 0 = 2 output[1] = input[1]*w[0] + input[2]*w[1] + input[3]*w[2] = 1 + 0 + 0 = 1 output[2] = input[2]*w[0] + input[3]*w[1] + input[4]*w[2] = 0 output[3] = 0 But earlier I thought output[0] should be 1! So the formula must be different. Let me look at how the transpose actually works mathematically. For Conv3d (cross-correlation) with stride=1, pad=0: y[i] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[i+k] * w[k] For input x of size N, output y of size N-K+1. The transpose operation computes the gradient w.r.t. x: dx[i] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} dy[i-k] * w[k] = sum_{k: 0 <= i-k < M} dy[i-k] * w[k] = sum_{k=max(0, i-M+1)}^{min(K-1, i)} dy[i-k] * w[k] Where M is the size of dy. For y = [3, 3] (M=2), w = [1,1,1]: dx[0] = dy[0]*w[0] = 3*1 = 3 dx[1] = dy[1]*w[0] + dy[0]*w[1] = 3 + 3 = 6 dx[2] = dy[1]*w[1] + dy[0]*w[2] = 3 + 3 = 6 dx[3] = dy[1]*w[2] = 3 So dx = [3, 6, 6, 3], not [1,1,1,1]! Ah, the key insight is that ConvTranspose3d is NOT the exact inverse of Conv3d. It's the transpose (adjoint) operation used in backpropagation. It only reconstructs the original input when the convolution preserves all information and the weights are the pseudo-inverse. So my earlier intuition about "reconstructing" was wrong. ConvTranspose3d computes the mathematical transpose operation. Now, for the actual formula used by PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d: For cross-correlation Conv3d: y[p] = sum_k x[p+k] * w[k] The transpose computes: x[i] += sum_k y[i-k] * w[k] For output position p in ConvTranspose3d: output[p] = sum_k input[p-k] * w[k] (with valid bounds) Or equivalently: for each input position i and each kernel position k: output[i + k] += input[i] * w[k] This is the "scatter-add" interpretation. For padding p in ConvTranspose3d: If the equivalent Conv3d has padding p, then the transpose conv maps the output back to the original input size. The formula with padding is: output[p] = sum_k input[p - k + p_pad] * w[k] Actually, the relationship for padding is: Conv3d with padding p pads the input with p zeros on each side. So the effective input has indices from -p to N+p-1, with 0s outside [0, N-1]. The transpose operation doesn't "add" output, it effectively has the same formula but computes a larger output. For ConvTranspose3d with padding=p: output[d] = sum_k input[d - k + p] * w[k] ... no wait. After looking at various sources more carefully, the actual PyTorch ConvTranspose3d formula for stride=1 is: output[d] = sum_k input[d - k + padding] * w[k] with 0 <= d - k + padding < input_size Or equivalently: input index = d - k + padding. Let me verify with p=1, input_size=2, kernel=3, stride=1: output_size = 2 - 1 - 2 + 3 = 2 output[0]: k=0: input[0-0+1] = input[1] = valid k=1: input[0-1+1] = input[0] = valid k=2: input[0-2+1] = input[-1] = invalid output[0] = input[1]*w[0] + input[0]*w[1] output[1]: k=0: input[1-0+1] = input[2] = invalid k=1: input[1-1+1] = input[1] = valid k=2: input[1-2+1] = input[0] = valid output[1] = input[1]*w[1] + input[0]*w[2] And for Conv3d with p=1, input_size=4, kernel=3, stride=1: output_size = 4 + 2 - 3 + 1 = 4 For input [a,b,c,d], p=1: The padded input is [0,a,b,c,d,0] output[0] = 0*w[0] + a*w[1] + b*w[2] output[1] = a*w[0] + b*w[1] + c*w[2] output[2] = b*w[0] + c*w[1] + d*w[2] output[3] = c*w[0] + d*w[1] + 0*w[2] The transpose of this 4×4 linear transform applied to a 2-element vector... Actually, the transpose conv issize-changing: it maps from output_size to input_size of the forward conv. For Conv3d(p=1) on 4-element input → 4-element output. The transpose would map 4-element input to 4-element output? Wait, no. The transpose operation maps from the output space to the input space of the forward operation. So it's 4→4 in this case. Actually for Conv3d(p=1, k=3, s=1) on N=4 input: output_size = (4 + 2*1 - 3) / 1 + 1 = 4. ConvTranspose3d with same params on 4-element input: output_size = (4 - 1) * 1 - 2*1 + 3 + 0 = 3. So they map different sizes. The transpose is not computing the exact inverse, it's computing the adjoint/transpose linear operator. For the adjoint of padded convolution: The forward conv on padded input: y[i] = sum_k padded_x[i+k] * w[k] where padded_x[j] = x[j-p] for p <= j < N+p, else 0. The transpose operation on y computes: grad_x[j] = sum_k y[j-k] * w[k] (this is for the padded input) grad_x[j] = sum_{k: j-k valid} y[j-k] * w[k] Taking into account the padding shift: For original input x, grad_x at position i in the original input corresponds to position j = i + p in the padded input. grad_x[i] = grad_x[i+p] = sum_k y[i+p-k] * w[k] So for the ConvTranspose3d output: output[i] = sum_k input[i + p - k] * w[k] where w is the original weight. Hmm wait, I'm still confused by the indices. Let me just run a mental check. If we have a Conv3d that maps from input x to output y, the transpose operation should be such that: <Conv3d(x), y> = <x, ConvTranspose3d(y)> For the inner product to work, the dimensions need to match. Actually, for Conv3d with p=0, k=3, s=1: x(N) → y(N-2) Transpose: y(M) → x(M+2) So ConvTranspose3d(y, p=0, k=3) gives output of size M+2. For the adjoint relationship: Conv3d(x, k=3)[i] = x[i]*w[0] + x[i+1]*w[1] + x[i+2]*w[2] <Conv3d(x), y> = sum_i (x[i]*w[0] + x[i+1]*w[1] + x[i+2]*w[2]) * y[i] = sum_i x[i] * (y[i]*w[0] + y[i-1]*w[1] + y[i-2]*w[2]) So ConvTranspose3d(y)[i] = y[i]*w[0] + y[i-1]*w[1] + y[i-2]*w[2] = sum_k y[i-k] * w[k] With y[-1] = y[-2] = 0 (out of bounds). So Conv3d(x)[i] = x[i-1]*w[0] + x[i]*w[1] + x[i+1]*w[2] for valid i where all indices are in [0, N-1]. For N=4: output[0] = x[-1]*w[0] + x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[2] = x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[2] output[1] = x[0]*w[0] + x[1]*w[1] + x[2]*w[2] output[2] = x[1]*w[0] + x[2]*w[1] + x[3]*w[2] output[3] = x[2]*w[0] + x[3]*w[1] + x[4]*w[2] = x[2]*w[0] + x[3]*w[1] <Conv3d(x), y> = sum_i (sum_j A[i,j] * x[j]) * y[i] = sum_j x[j] * (sum_i A[i,j] * y[i]) Where A[i,j] is the matrix such that Conv3d(x)[i] = sum_j A[i,j] * x[j]. For Conv3d(p=1, k=3): A[0,0] = w[1], A[0,1] = w[2] A[1,0] = w[0], A[1,1] = w[1], A[1,2] = w[2] A[2,1] = w[0], A[2,2] = w[1], A[2,3] = w[2] A[3,2] = w[0], A[3,3] = w[1] <A^T y>[j] = sum_i A[i,j] * y[i] <A^T y>[0] = A[0,0]*y[0] + A[1,0]*y[1] = w[1]*y[0] + w[0]*y[1] <A^T y>[1] = A[0,1]*y[0] + A[1,1]*y[1] + A[2,1]*y[2] = w[2]*y[0] + w[1]*y[1] + w[0]*y[2] <A^T y>[2] = A[1,2]*y[1] + A[2,2]*y[2] + A[3,2]*y[3] = w[2]*y[1] + w[1]*y[2] + w[0]*y[3] <A^T y>[3] = A[2,3]*y[2] + A[3,3]*y[3] = w[2]*y[2] + w[1]*y[3] So ConvTranspose3d(y, p=1, k=3) gives 4 outputs. Grid: (spatial_tiles, B, (OC + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE) For spatial_tiles = tiles_x * tiles_y * tiles_z: With 66×66×66 output, TX=8, TY=8, TZ=4: tiles_x = ceil(66/8) = 9 tiles_y = ceil(66/8) = 9 tiles_z = ceil(66/4) = 17 spatial_tiles = 9 * 9 * 17 = 1377 Grid.z = ceil(48/8) = 6 Total blocks = 1377 * B * 6. No, there's only ONE block per (spatial_tile, batch, oc_group). For spatial tile 0, batch 0, oc_group 0: - The block computes channels 0-7. - Within the tile, warp 0 computes 32 positions for channel 0. - Warp 1 computes 32 positions for channel 1. - etc. But what about the other 224 positions for channel 0? They're never computed! OH NO. This is exactly the bug. Each channel within a block only gets 32 out of 256 positions computed. The other positions are left uninitialized. Since we use `torch::empty`, the uninitialized memory contains garbage. So the fix is to ensure each thread/warp computes ALL positions for its assigned channel, or to distribute the work properly. Options: 1. Have each warp compute all 256 positions by iterating. 256/32 = 8 iterations per warp. 2. Have all 8 warps work together on one channel (each doing 32 positions), and iterate over channels. 3. Change design so each thread computes multiple output channels. Let me redesign the kernel. I'll go with a simpler approach first: Each thread computes one spatial position (oz, oy, ox) for ALL output channels. - Block handles TX × TY × TZ spatial positions - Each thread loops over OC channels - BlockDim.x = TX * TY * TZ Actually, to optimize shared memory, a better design: - Block handles a spatial tile and a subset of OC channels - Load input tile into shared memory (expanded for halo) - Load weight tile into shared memory - Each thread computes one or more (oz, oy, ox, oc) positions But for simplicity and correctness, let me do: - Each block handles a (TX, TY, TZ) spatial tile and OC_TILE output channels - Block size = TX * TY * TZ threads - Each thread computes one spatial position for all OC_TILE channels With TX=8, TY=8, TZ=4: block size = 256 threads. Each thread handles 8 output channels. Total work per block = 256 spatial × 8 channels = 2048 values. For OC=48, we'd need 48/8 = 6 blocks per spatial tile. Grid = (spatial_tiles, B, OC_blocks) This is naturally correct because each thread writes all channels for one spatial position. Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, ... ) { // Spatial tile int tx = threadIdx.x % TX; int ty = (threadIdx.x / TX) % TY; int tz = threadIdx.x / (TX * TY); int bx = blockIdx.x % tiles_x; int by = (blockIdx.x / tiles_x) % tiles_y; int bz = blockIdx.x / (tiles_x * tiles_y); int ox = bx * TX + tx; int oy = by * TY + ty; int oz = bz * TZ + tz; int b = blockIdx.y; int oc_start = blockIdx.z * OC_TILE; if (ox >= OW || oy >= OH || oz >= OD) return; float accum[OC_TILE]; // or use explicit unrolling for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input tile with halo into smem // Load weight tile into smem for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { int oc = oc_start + oc_off; if (oc >= OC) continue; float sum = 0; for (int ic_off = 0; ic_off < IC_TILE && ic_base + ic_off < IC; ++ic_off) { for (int kz = 0; kz < KD; ++kz) { int iz = oz - kz + padding; for (int ky = 0; ky < KH; ++ky) { int iy = oy - ky + padding; for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix = ox - kx + padding; if (iz < 0 || iz >= ID || iy < 0 || iy >= IH || ix < 0 || ix >= IW) continue; float in_val = input[...]; float w_val = weight[...]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } accum[oc_off] += sum; } } // Write output for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { int oc = oc_start + oc_off; if (oc >= OC) continue; output[...] = accum[oc_off]; } } ``` Hmm, but register pressure might be high if we use a register array for OC_TILE=8. Let me actually keep OC_TILE smaller, like 1 or 2. Actually, for the conv transpose with moderate OC=48 and IC=48, and small kernel 3×3×3=27, the computation per output is 48*48*27 = 62,208 mul-adds. That's a lot, so we definitely want to maximize parallelism. For the H100, with 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth and 67 TFLOPS compute, this problem is compute-bound (62K FLOPs per output element, 48*66^3 ≈ 13.8M outputs, total ~860 GFLOPs - actually that's a lot, maybe bandwidth is not the bottleneck but instruction throughput is). Actually, the cleanest design: - Each thread computes ONE (oz, oy, ox, oc) output - Grid covers all output elements - No shared memory, direct global memory access (simplest, but slow) - Then optimize with shared memory But to be fast, we need shared memory. Let me do: Design 2: - Block handles (TX, TY, TZ) = (8, 8, 4) spatial tile and OC_TILE=8 channels - Total threads = TX * TY * TZ = 256 - But we need to compute for all 8 channels at all 256 positions - That's 2048 output values per block - Each thread computes 8 output values (one per channel) for one spatial position - Or: each thread computes 1 output value, and we need 2048 threads... but we only have 256 Option: thread computes ALL 8 channels for 1 spatial position. Register pressure: 8 accumulators. That's fine. ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { int tx = threadIdx.x % TX; int ty = (threadIdx.x / TX) % TY; int tz = threadIdx.x / (TX * TY); int tile_x = blockIdx.x % tiles_x; int tile_y = (blockIdx.x / tiles_x) % tiles_y; int tile_z = blockIdx.x / (tiles_x * tiles_y); int ox = tile_x * TX + tx; int oy = tile_y * TY + ty; int oz = tile_z * TZ + tz; int b = blockIdx.y; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OC_TILE; if (ox >= OW || oy >= OH || oz >= OD) return; float accum[8] = {0}; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { // Load input for this IC at all relevant kernel positions // Or just load directly from global memory for now for (int kz = 0; kz < KD; ++kz) { int iz = oz - kz + padding; if (iz < 0 || iz >= ID) continue; for (int ky = 0; ky < KH; ++ky) { int iy = oy - ky + padding; if (iy < 0 || iy >= IH) continue; for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix = ox - kx + padding; if (ix < 0 || ix >= IW) continue; float in_val = input[(((b * IC + ic) * ID + iz) * IH + iy) * IW + ix]; #pragma unroll for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { int oc = oc_base + oc_off; if (oc >= OC) continue; float w_val = weight[(((ic * OC + oc) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx]; accum[oc_off] += in_val * w_val; } } } } } #pragma unroll for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { int oc = oc_base + oc_off; if (oc >= OC) continue; output[(((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH + oy) * OW + ox] = accum[oc_off]; } } ``` With OC_TILE=8, each thread has 8 accumulators, and the inner loop over kernel positions updates all 8. The register pressure is manageable. To optimize, I should add shared memory: - For a spatial tile, the relevant input region depends on the tile and padding. - Without padding and with KD=3, a (8,8,4) tile needs input from positions [ox-2, ox] to [ox+7], etc. Actually for the formula output[ox] = sum_k input[ox - k + p] * w[k]: For ox in [ox_base, ox_base+TX-1], the needed input ix is in [ox_base - KW + 1 + p, ox_base + TX - 1 + p]. Size = TX + KW - 1. For the loop over ic, we can load a tile of input into shared memory for each ic. But since IC=48, we'd need many iterations. A more efficient approach: load input for IC_TILE input channels at once. Let me think about this differently. Since the kernel is 3×3×3=27 and IC=48, OC=48, the weight is 48*48*27 = 62,208 floats = 248KB. That fits in shared memory if loaded incrementally. For IC_TILE=8: - Input tile size: IC_TILE * (TX+KW-1) * (TY+KH-1) * (TZ+KD-1) = 8 * 10 * 10 * 6 = 4800 floats = 19KB - Weight tile: IC_TILE * OC_TILE * 27 = 8 * 8 * 27 = 1728 floats = 7KB - Total per iteration: ~26KB. Fits easily in 228KB shared memory. This is exactly what I was trying to do before! But the previous kernel had a bug where each warp only computed 1/8th of the spatial tile. Let me now redesign properly. The key fix: each thread computes ALL OC_TILE channels for ONE spatial position. ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel(...) { int tx = threadIdx.x % TX; int ty = (threadIdx.x / TX) % TY; int tz = threadIdx.x / (TX * TY); // ... decode block indices ... int ox = tile_x * TX + tx; int oy = tile_y * TY + ty; int oz = tile_z * TZ + tz; int b = blockIdx.y; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OC_TILE; if (ox >= OW || oy >= OH || oz >= OD) return; float accum[OC_TILE] = {0}; for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input tile (with halo) into shared memory __shared__ float in_smem[IC_TILE][FZ][FY][FX]; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < IC_TILE * FZ * FY * FX; i += blockDim.x) { // decode i to (ic_off, iz, iy, ix) in smem int ic_off = i / (FZ * FY * FX); int rem = i % (FZ * FY * FX); int iz_local = rem / (FY * FX); int rem2 = rem % (FY * FX); int iy_local = rem2 / FX; int ix_local = rem2 % FX; int g_iz = tile_z * TZ + iz_local - (KD - 1) + padding; int g_iy = tile_y * TY + iy_local - (KH - 1) + padding; int g_ix = tile_x * TX + ix_local - (KW - 1) + padding; float val = 0.0f; if (g_iz >= 0 && g_iz < ID && g_iy >= 0 && g_iy < IH && g_ix >= 0 && g_ix < IW) { val = input[(((b * IC + ic_base + ic_off) * ID + g_iz) * IH + g_iy) * IW + g_ix]; } in_smem[ic_off][iz_local][iy_local][ix_local] = val; } // Load weight tile into shared memory __shared__ float w_smem[IC_TILE][OC_TILE][KD][KH][KW]; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < IC_TILE * OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW; i += blockDim.x) { int ic_off = i / (OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW); int rem = i % (OC_TILE * KD * KH * KW); int oc_off = rem / (KD * KH * KW); int k_rem = rem % (KD * KH * KW); int kz = k_rem / (KH * KW); int k_rem2 = k_rem % (KH * KW); int ky = k_rem2 / KW; int kx = k_rem2 % KW; int global_oc = oc_base + oc_off; int global_ic = ic_base + ic_off; float val = 0.0f; if (global_oc < OC && global_ic < IC) { val = weight[(((global_ic * OC + global_oc) * KD + kz) * KH + ky) * KW + kx]; } w_smem[ic_off][oc_off][kz][ky][kx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute for this thread's spatial position for (int ic_off = 0; ic_off < IC_TILE && ic_base + ic_off < IC; ++ic_off) { for (int kz = 0; kz < KD; ++kz) { int iz_local = tz + (KD - 1) - kz; // Because g_iz = tile_z*TZ + iz_local - (KD-1) + p for (int ky = 0; ky < KH; ++ky) { int iy_local = ty + (KH - 1) - ky; for (int kx = 0; kx < KW; ++kx) { int ix_local = tx + (KW - 1) - kx; float in_val = in_smem[ic_off][iz_local][iy_local][ix_local]; #pragma unroll for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { float w_val = w_smem[ic_off][oc_off][kz][ky][kx]; accum[oc_off] += in_val * w_val; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output for (int oc_off = 0; oc_off < OC_TILE; ++oc_off) { int oc = oc_base + oc_off; if (oc >= OC) continue; output[(((b * OC + oc) * OD + oz) * OH + oy) * OW + ox] = accum[oc_off]; } } ``` Wait, a subtle issue: when I iterate over `oc_off` in the compute loop with `#pragma unroll`, but `OC_TILE` might be a template parameter or known constant. If it's fixed at 8, the compiler can unroll. But it's declared as a compile-time constant in my current design. Actually, I need OC_TILE to be a compile-time constant for the array `float accum[OC_TILE]`. In CUDA, you can use `constexpr int OC_TILE = 8;` for this. Let me write the full kernel with this fixed design. I'll make sure to use compile-time constants for the tile sizes to allow array declarations and better unrolling. Actually, there's another concern: with `float accum[8]`, the compiler might put this in registers but with 256 threads and 8 registers each... that's fine. Also, the shared memory declaration as multi-dimensional arrays with sizes that are template parameters is fine with CUDA. Let me write the corrected kernel. One more check on the spatial indexing: - We have a tile of TX × TY × TZ output positions. - For each output position (ox = tile_x*TX + tx, oy = tile_y*TY + ty, oz = tile_z*TZ + tz): - We need input at (ox - kx + p, oy - ky + p, oz - kz + p). The shared memory input region covers: ix in [tile_x*TX - (KW-1) + p, tile_x*TX + TX - 1 + p] Which maps to local ix_s in [0, TX + KW - 2]. Wait, the size is TX + KW - 1 (not TX + KW - 2). For TX=8, KW=3: size = 8 + 3 - 1 = 10. Local ix_s = 0 corresponds to g_ix = tile_x*TX - (KW-1) + p = tile_x*TX - 2 + p. Global ix = tile_x*TX + tx - kx + p. Local ix_s = global_ix - (tile_x*TX - 2 + p) = tx - kx + 2 = tx + (KW-1) - kx. So ix_s = tx + (KW - 1) - kx. This matches! For the bounds check in the compute loop: we need to make sure ix_s, iy_s, iz_s are valid. ix_s = tx + 2 - kx, where tx in [0, TX-1], kx in [0, KW-1]. Minimum: 0 + 2 - 2 = 0. Maximum: 7 + 2 - 0 = 9. And FX = TX + KW - 1 = 10, so indices 0-9 are valid. ✓ Same for the others. Great, this design should be correct. Let me now write the full code. For the template parameters: ```cpp template <int TX=8, int TY=8, int TZ=4, int OC_TILE=8, int IC_TILE=8> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel(...) { constexpr int FZ = TZ + KD - 1; // Wait, KD is a runtime parameter // KD is passed at runtime, so FZ is not a constant expression } ``` Hmm, KD is passed as a runtime parameter, so I can't use it in `constexpr`. In my original kernel, I computed FZ as `TZ + KD - 1` which is fine for indexing but not for array declarations. For shared memory, I can use `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and manual indexing. Or I can compute the shared memory sizes dynamically and pass to the kernel launch. Actually, since KD=KH=KW=3 for the test case, I can use compile-time constants for the kernel sizes too. But to be general, let me use the dynamic shared memory approach. For the `accum[OC_TILE]` array, OC_TILE is a compile-time constant (like 8), so that's fine. Let me write the kernel now.

For output position (d, h, w), it receives contributions from all input positions (d', h', w') where the kernel could reach it. No wait, that's for a regular convolution with flipped kernel. For transpose, let's think about it as the adjoint of a strided convolution. If we have a regular conv C with stride S, padding P: (C * x)[i] = sum_j K[j] * x[S*i + j - P] For conv transpose C^T: (C^T * y)[k] = sum_i K[k - S*i + P] * y[i] for valid i where k - S*i + P is in [0, K-1]. For S=1, P=0: (C^T * y)[k] = sum_i K[k - i] * y[i] = sum_j K[j] * y[k - j] where j = k - i. So output[k] = sum_j K[j] * input[k - j]. This means for output position k: - j=0: K[0] * input[k] - j=1: K[1] * input[k-1] - j=2: K[2] * input[k-2] So output[k] = K[0]*input[k] + K[1]*input[k-1] + K[2]*input[k-2] Wait, but this would give: output[0] = K[0]*input[0] output[1] = K[0]*input[1] + K[1]*input[0] output[2] = K[0]*input[2] + K[1]*input[1] + K[2]*input[0] This is exactly what I computed earlier! And it matches my formula: output[d] = sum_kd weight[kd] * input[d - kd] But wait, I need to double check PyTorch's convention. Is it `weight[kd] * input[d - kd]` or `weight[K-1-kd] * input[d - kd]`? In a regular conv with no stride, padding, the formula is: output[i] = sum_j weight[j] * input[i + j - padding] For conv transpose, which is equivalent to convolving with a dilated input, the formula should be: For stride=1 conv transpose with no padding, output size = input_size + kernel_size - 1. And the formula is indeed: output[n] = sum_k weight[k] * input[n - k] (with appropriate bounds) So my formula should be correct. BUT WAIT! Let me check - is PyTorch's `ConvTranspose3d` actually computing the transpose of the convolution matrix, or is it doing something slightly different? From the PyTorch docs: > This module can be seen as the gradient of Conv3d with respect to its input. If Conv3d computes output = conv(input, weight), then ConvTranspose3d computes output = conv_transpose(input, weight). For Conv3d with stride=1, padding=0: output[i] = sum_j weight[j] * input[i + j] (where we use "valid" convolution) For the transpose: If we consider Conv3d as a matrix M where M[i, j] = weight[j - i] (for appropriate ranges), then M^T[j, i] = M[i, j] = weight[j - i]. So (M^T * y)[j] = sum_i M^T[j, i] * y[i] = sum_i weight[j - i] * y[i]. This gives output[j] = sum_i weight[j - i] * input[i]. Setting k = j - i, we get i = j - k: output[j] = sum_k weight[k] * input[j - k]. This is the same formula! ✓ So my formula is mathematically correct for the PyTorch interpretation of conv transpose. Actually, let me think about whether there could be an issue with the number of grid blocks. Could there be integer overflow? ``` int tiles_x = (OW + TX - 1) / TX; ``` For OW=66, TX=8: tiles_x = ceil(66/8) = 9. spatial_tiles = 9 * 9 * 17 = 1377. grid.x = 1377, which fits in int. ✓ Block x dimension: ``` dim3 block(TX * TY * TZ); ``` = 256 threads. Max threads per block is 1024. ✓ Could there be a synchronization issue? Shared memory loads should be safe with __syncthreads(). But what if there are threads that don't participate in the __syncthreads? All threads in a block participate because there are no divergent paths before __syncthreads(). The `if (ox >= OW || ... ) return;` check is AFTER the computation, so all threads reach __syncthreads(). ✓ Wait, actually, that check is BEFORE the loop! Let me look: ``` if (ox >= OW || oy >= OH || oz >= OD) return; ``` This is at the very beginning! So threads with invalid positions return immediately, BEFORE reaching __syncthreads()! This is a fatal bug! If some threads in a block return early while others continue, then when the remaining threads hit __syncthreads(), they'll wait forever (or cause undefined behavior) because the returned threads never reach it! CUDA's __syncthreads() requires ALL threads in a block to reach it. If some threads return early (due to the if statement at the beginning), the block will deadlock or produce incorrect results. THIS IS THE BUG! For the last spatial tile: - tile_x=8: ox ranges 64..71. Valid ow=66, so tx=0,1 give valid ox=64,65. tx=2..7 give ox=66..71 which are >= 66, so those threads return early. - Similarly for ty: tile_y=8, ty=0,1 are valid, ty=2..7 return. - For tz: tile_z=16, tz=0,1 are valid (oz=64,65), tz=2,3 return (oz=66,67 >= OD=66). So in the last tile, many threads return early. For example, threads with (tx=2, any ty, any tz) return immediately. Then when the remaining threads try to do __syncthreads(), they hang because those threads never reach it! This is exactly the issue! On modern GPUs, threads that don't reach __syncthreads() will cause the block to deadlock or the warps to diverge incorrectly. I need to move the bounds check to AFTER the shared memory loads and synchronization, or better, ensure all threads participate in the shared memory loads and only skip the computation/write. Let me fix this. I'll remove the early return and use a flag or just compute with 0-padding then skip the write. Actually, the easiest fix is to let all threads participate in shared memory loads and __syncthreads(), and only skip computation/write. But then invalid threads would load out-of-bounds memory for input. Better approach: for the input and weight loads, still have all threads participate but use conditional bounds checking (which they already do). The `if (g_iz >= 0 && ...)` checks already handle this - out-of-bounds reads get 0. And for weight loads, out-of-bounds get 0 too. Then all threads reach __syncthreads(). Then in the compute phase, we can have a condition `if (ox >= OW || oy >= OH || oz >= OD)` before the compute loop to skip computation for those threads. Then another __syncthreads(). And all threads must also reach the second __syncthreads(). Wait, but in the current code, there's a __syncthreads inside the IC loop. The invalid threads must also reach this __syncthreads. Let me restructure: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { // Compute thread coordinates // All threads participate in shared memory loads for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input into smem (with bounds checks) // Load weight into smem (with bounds checks) __syncthreads(); // Only valid threads do computation if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD) { // compute accum } __syncthreads(); } // Only valid threads write output if (ox < OW && oy < OH && oz < OD) { // write accum } } ``` This way all threads reach every __syncthreads(), avoiding deadlock. Let me rewrite the kernel with this structure.
assistantI found the bug: threads that fall outside the output bounds return early, **before** `__syncthreads()`, which causes deadlock/UB because CUDA requires all threads in a block to reach every barrier. Let me fix that by moving the bounds check after the synchronization points:

Actually, looking at the cuDNN convolution transpose formula: CUDNN uses the relationship `y = convTranspose(x, w)` where `w` has shape `(C_out, C_in, K, K, K)` for regular convolution. For transpose convolution, cuDNN effectively does `y = conv(x, w_rotated)` where `w_rotated` is spatially flipped. But PyTorch handles this transparently. Hmm, but let me consider whether PyTorch flips the kernel internally. Actually, I recall now: `nn.ConvTranspose3d` in PyTorch computes the transposed convolution which is equivalent to a full convolution with the spatially **flipped** weight. That is, the transposed convolution of stride=1 and padding=0 is equivalent to `conv(input, flip(weight))` with appropriate padding. Wait, no. Let me be precise. - Standard convolution: `out[i] = sum_j in[i+j] * w[j]` - Transposed convolution: `out[i] = sum_j in[i-j] * w[j]` = `sum_j in[j] * w[j-i]` The transposed convolution with weight `w` is the same as convolution with flipped weight `w[-i]` but with different bounds. For the 1D case: - Transpose conv: `out[n] = sum_m input[n-m] * weight[m]` for valid m - This is equivalent to cross-correlation: `out[n] = sum_m input[n+m] * flip(weight)[m]` Where `flip(weight)[m] = weight[-m]`. If weight has indices 0..K-1, then flip(weight)[m] = weight[K-1-m]. Actually for standard conv in signal processing (cross-correlation): `out[n] = sum_m input[n+m] * w[m]` For transpose conv: `out[n] = sum_m input[n-m] * w[m] = sum_m input[n+m] * w[-m]` But with finite support, if w is indexed from 0 to K-1, then w[-m] for m negative doesn't exist unless we flip the kernel. Actually, I think the correct relationship is: Transposed conv with weight w is equivalent to conv (as in direct sum) with weight w. Specifically for stride=1 and no padding: `out[n] = sum_m input[n-m] * w[m]` Butimplemented in nn.ConvTranspose3d, the weight is directly used in the spatially unflipped form in certain frameworks after appropriate transformations. PyTorch uses cuDNN which handles this internally. The weights are NOT spatially flipped by PyTorch before being passed to cuDNN. So cuDNN computes: `y[n] = sum_m x[n-m] * w[m]` (the definition). And my code computes: `y[n] += sum_m x[n-m] * w[m]`. PyTorch docs for `ConvTranspose3d`: > The transposed convolution operator multiplies each input value element-wise by a learnable kernel, and sums over the outputs from all input feature planes. > This module can be seen as the gradient of Conv3d with respect to its input. It is also known as a fractionally-strided convolution or a deconvolution (although it is not an actual deconvolution operation as it does not compute a true inverse of convolution. The output is only inverse in the sense that it has the same spatial dimensions as the input to Conv3d for the same kernel size, stride and padding). For the exact formula, PyTorch computes: ``` out(N_i, C_out, d, h, w) = bias(C_out) + sum_k (C_in) weight(C_out, k, d, h, w) * input(N_i, k, d', h', w') ``` where `d'` depends on stride, padding, dilation, and kernel size. Let me check... Actually, I think the formula might be different. Let me look at the docs more carefully. For Conv2d (and 3d), the formula is: ``` out(N_i, C_out, h, w) = bias(C_out) + sum_{C_in} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} weight(C_out, C_in, kh, kw) * input(N_i, C_in, stride*h + kh - pad, stride*w + kw - pad) ``` For ConvTranspose2d: ``` out(N_i, C_out, h, w) = bias(C_out) + sum_{C_in} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} weight(C_in, C_out, kh, kw) * input(N_i, C_in, (h + pad - kh) / stride, ...) ``` Hmm, the formula for conv_transpose is tricky because of the strided relationship. A known fact is that ConvTranspose2d with stride=1 and padding=0 computes the full convolution: ``` out[h, w] = sum_{kh, kw} in[h - kh, w - kw] * weight[kh, kw] ``` where out-of-bounds input is treated as 0. Wait, but is it `h - kh` or `h + kh`? Let me think about this carefully. Consider a 1D regular conv with stride=1, kernel size 3, no padding: ``` out[0] = in[0] * w[0] + in[1] * w[1] + in[2] * w[2] out[1] = in[1] * w[0] + in[2] * w[1] + in[3] * w[2] ``` Here, input indices for out[i] go from i to i+K-1. Now if we want the transpose operation, we want to find an operation that maps a gradient at the output back to the input. For gradient wrt input in regular conv: ``` dL/din[j] = sum_i dL/dout[i] * d(out[i])/d(in[j]) = sum_{i: j in [i, i+K-1]} dL/dout[i] * w[j-i] = sum_{kd} dL/dout[j - kd] * w[kd] ``` So for the transpose operation: ``` out[j] = sum_{kd} in[j - kd] * w[kd] ``` where `kd` ranges such that `j - kd` is a valid input index, i.e., `kd` from `max(0, j - (N_in - 1))` to `min(K - 1, j)`. This matches what I computed! My formula: ``` accum += input[oz - kz] * weight[kz] ``` So it seems correct. Actually, wait. PyTorch's conv_transpose is defined with flipped weight in some implementations. Let me check if the weight `w[kd]` should be `w[K-1-kd]`. When we compute `grad_input[j] = sum_i grad_output[i] * w[j - i]`, the `w` index is `j - i`. For a regular conv, the weight at index `kd` corresponds to `in[i + kd]`, so `w[kd]` multiplies `in[i + kd]`. The partial derivative of `out[i]` wrt `in[j]` is `w[j - i]` when `0 <= j - i < K`. So grad wrt in[j] = sum_i grad_out[i] * w[j - i]. Now, j - i can be written as kd. When i = j - kd: grad_in[j] = sum_kd grad_out[j - kd] * w[kd]. This is `in[j] -> sum_kd out[j-kd] * w[kd]`. For the transpose operation to be the forward pass of ConvTranspose, we have: `ConvTranspose(input, w)[j] = sum_kd input[j - kd] * w[kd]`. So the weight index is `kd` directly, not flipped. My code uses `weight[kd]` directly which is correct. But wait, this is a subtle point. Let me think about if PyTorch actually stores the weight in a spatially-reversed manner for ConvTranspose3d. I don't think so—the weight is stored in its natural order, and the transpose operation computes the mathematical transpose of the convolution.
